    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="99541faad0b840b8bd9b36de2cd3a743">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '287841ec-07af-48fb-9122-b8579257395c'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d173147810" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>As the preferred anode material for sodium-ion batteries, hard carbon (HC) confronts significant obstacles in providing a long and dominant low-voltage plateau to boost the output energy density of full batteries. The critical challenge lies in precisely enhancing the local graphitization degree to minimize Na<sup>+</sup> ad-/chemisorption, while effectively controlling the growth of internal closed nanopores to maximize Na<sup>+</sup> filling. Unfortunately, traditional high-temperature preparation methods struggle to achieve both objectives simultaneously. Herein, a transient sintering-involved kinetically-controlled synthesis strategy is proposed that enables the creation of metastable HCs with precisely tunable carbon phases and low discharge/charge voltage plateaus. By optimizing the temperature and width of thermal pulses, the high-throughput screened HCs are characterized by short-range ordered graphitic micro-domains that possess accurate crystallite width and height, as well as appropriately-sized closed nanopores. This advancement realizes HC anodes with significantly prolonged low-voltage plateaus below 0.1&nbsp;V, with the best sample exhibiting a high plateau capacity of up to 325&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The energy density of the HC||Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full battery can therefore be increased by 20.7%. Machine learning study explicitly unveils the “carbon phase evolution−electrochemistry” relationship. This work promises disruptive changes to the synthesis, optimization, and commercialization of HC anodes for high-energy-density sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202407369-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202407369-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are emerging as the most promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in large-scale energy storage and low-speed electric vehicles owing to their low cost,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0001_R_d173147792e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> abundant resource reserves, and low-temperature applicability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0002_R_d173147792e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">2</a>-<a href="#adma202407369-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0008_R_d173147792e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Currently, SIBs are still in the exploration stage of active material screening and battery system optimization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0009_R_d173147792e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Energy">9</a>-<a href="#adma202407369-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0013_R_d173147792e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In terms of anode materials, hard carbon (HC) has risen above its competitors as the preferred choice for SIBs owing to its comprehensive performance advantages.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0014_R_d173147792e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a>-<a href="#adma202407369-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0017_R_d173147792e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Structurally, HC is a highly complex carbon alloy composed of randomly-oriented pseudo-graphitic micro-domains, amorphous regions, and open/closed nanopores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0018_R_d173147792e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Mater. Today">18</a>, <a href="#adma202407369-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0019_R_d173147792e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The structural complexity inevitably determines the diversity of sodium ion storage forms in HC anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0020_R_d173147792e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite ongoing debates,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0021_R_d173147792e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a>-<a href="#adma202407369-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0024_R_d173147792e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> there is a general consensus that the sodium storage in HC basically follows the “adsorption-filling” mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0025_R_d173147792e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Energy">25</a>, <a href="#adma202407369-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0026_R_d173147792e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On one hand, the ad-/chemisorption of Na<sup>+</sup> ions at defect sites and open surfaces gives rise to a slope region in the high-voltage interval between 1.0 and 0.1&nbsp;V, and normally accounts for more than 30% of the overall capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0027_R_d173147792e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a>, <a href="#adma202407369-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0028_R_d173147792e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the other hand, the filling of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into closed nanopores in a quasi-metallic state brings about a low-voltage plateau region below 0.1&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0029_R_d173147792e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a>-<a href="#adma202407369-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0032_R_d173147792e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - NPJ Comput.Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, HC shows a strong precursor dependence on its carbon microstructures and heteroatoms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0023_R_d173147792e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Environ. Sci.">23</a>, <a href="#adma202407369-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0033_R_d173147792e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a>, <a href="#adma202407369-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0034_R_d173147792e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This essentially leads to significant differences in slope region and plateau region capacities of HCs obtained from diverse biomass feedstocks.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0034_R_d173147792e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#adma202407369-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0035_R_d173147792e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Energy Environ. Sci.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>From the perspective of full batteries, people prefer HCs to offer long and dominant low-voltage discharge/charge plateaus like the graphite anode in LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0036_R_d173147792e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Mater. Technol.">36</a>-<a href="#adma202407369-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0038_R_d173147792e605" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is because the high-voltage slope region of HC is detrimental to both the output cell voltage and energy density of SIB full batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0039_R_d173147792e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Energy Environ. Sci.">39</a>, <a href="#adma202407369-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0040_R_d173147792e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This raises a very important scientific question: how to maximize plateau capacity below 0.1&nbsp;V and minimize slope region capacity by precisely modulating the various carbon phases (including crystalline carbon, amorphous carbon, and nanopores) in HCs?</p>
         
         <p>High-temperature sintering is the most direct and universal approach for regulating the microstructures of carbonaceous materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0041_R_d173147792e618" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When subjected to elevated temperatures, the pseudo-graphitic micro-domains in HCs become more long-range ordered, while the proportion of amorphous carbon decreases, accompanied by a reduction in defect levels.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0022_R_d173147792e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Environ. Sci.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Apparently, this is highly beneficial in reducing the Na<sup>+</sup> ad-/chemisorption at defect sites and diminishing the slope region capacity. However, high-temperature treatment is a “double-edged sword” for HCs, that is, when the slope region capacity decays to a certain threshold, the plateau capacity, and the overall capacity will decline sharply accordingly. This is because the carbon phase transition from amorphous to crystalline will promote the closed pore growth inside the HCs. With the increase in pore size, the nanopores will gradually become partially available or even unfillable for Na<sup>+</sup> ions owing to a combination of factors such as weakened induced nucleation activity, limited Na<sup>+</sup> conductivity of high-crystallinity graphene walls, as well as low thermodynamic probability of larger quasi-metallic cluster formation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0042_R_d173147792e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Energy Mater.">42</a>-<a href="#adma202407369-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0045_R_d173147792e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the current heat treatment of HCs has to find a trade-off between crystallinity and pore structure at moderate temperatures (1400–1500&nbsp;<sup>°</sup>C), which is manifested in commercial HCs retaining considerable slope region capacity to avoid severe plateau capacity loss.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0046_R_d173147792e643" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Chem. Lett.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Very recently, a few special precursors containing easy graphitizable components, for example, charcoal,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0047_R_d173147792e651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Sci. Bull.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> bamboo,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0048_R_d173147792e656" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Chem. Eng. J.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and wood rich in crystalline cellulose,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0049_R_d173147792e661" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Nat. Commun.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been screened out and broke through the inherent contradiction between graphitization and pore growth of HCs, resulting in the plateau region-dominated HC anodes. Impressively, Chou et&nbsp;al. achieved all-plateau and high-capacity topological graphitized carbon by utilizing corn cobs as the precursor.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0050_R_d173147792e667" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although these isolated cases are highly precursor dependent, they all indicate that easily graphitized components can form locally highly graphitized regions at lower temperatures, thus guaranteeing the formation of usable nanopores of appropriate size.</p>
         
         <p>To fundamentally solve the mutual constraint between graphitization degree enhancement and nanopore growth, and get rid of the dependence on precursors, here we have designed and introduced modified flash Joule heating technology in the preparation of metastable HC anodes for the first time, transforming the modulation of carbon phases inside HCs from a thermodynamically controlled process to a kinetically controlled process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202407369-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0051_R_d173147792e675" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Nature">51</a>-<a href="#adma202407369-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202407369-bib-0054_R_d173147792e675" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Nat. Commun.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By precisely controlling the temperature and width of transient thermal pulses, we can accurately regulate the local graphitization degree and closed pore growth of HCs within seconds, obtaining HCs with both short-range ordered graphitic micro-domains and appropriately sized closed nanopores (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202407369-fig-0001">1a,b</a>). More encouragingly, these kinetically controlled HC products can well persist over long periods and show a significantly prolonged low-working-voltage plateaus below 0.1&nbsp;V, with the best sample exhibiting a high plateau capacity of up to 325&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (89.5% of overall capacity, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202407369-fig-0001">1c</a>), which is at the forefront of current research in whole-plateau hard carbon. Most importantly, this high-plateau-capacity HC enables us to construct HC || Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full batteries with an energy density increment of up to 20.7%. This technique is universally applicable and precursor-independent, allowing for the precise regulation of both commercialized and independently synthesized HCs. We experimentally and theoretically elucidate the structural evolution of carbon phases and closed nanopores under pulses of different widths, as well as their correlation with sodium storage electrochemistry. Our work provides transformative solutions for the structural optimization and performance breakthrough of various carbonaceous electrode materials in the field of sustainable energy storage/conversion devices.</p>
